You said
Give me more detail on form for suitcase row

For a 6'6" lifter, the Suitcase Row is actually one of the best ways to train your back while protecting your spine, because it allows you to find a "neutral" position that a barbell sometimes restricts.

Since you're using a suitcase in a hotel, your biggest challenge is preventing your long torso from rotating or "rounding" as you pull.

1. The Setup: "The Three-Point Stance"
To take the pressure off that "dull" lower back pain, do not perform this standing unsupported.

The Support: Place your left hand firmly on the hotel bed or a sturdy desk.

The Feet: Stagger your stance. If you are rowing with your right hand, your left foot should be forward.

The Spine: Your back should be a straight line from your head to your tailbone, nearly parallel to the floor.

2. The Movement: "The Path to the Hip"
Most people pull the weight straight up to their chest. At your height, this "bunches up" the shoulder and puts stress on the neck.

The Arc: Think about pulling the suitcase handle back toward your hip, not up toward your armpit. Imagine there is a string attached to your elbow pulling it toward the wall behind you.

The Shoulder Blade: Start the movement by "setting" your shoulder blade—pull it back and down toward your spine before your arm even bends.

The Top: At the top of the move, your forearm should be vertical, and your elbow should be tucked close to your ribs, not flaring out.

3. Preventing the "Twist" (Anti-Rotation)
Because you have a long wingspan, the suitcase will want to pull your right shoulder toward the floor.

The Core: Squeeze your abs as if someone is about to punch you.

The "Square" Rule: Keep both shoulders square to the floor at all times. If your rowing shoulder is dipping lower than your supporting shoulder, the weight is too heavy or you are losing core tension.

4. The Modification for Hotel Luggage
If it's light: Use a 3-second hold at the top. Squeeze your shoulder blade against your spine as hard as you can.

If it's awkward: If the suitcase is too bulky and hits your legs, stand on a small elevation (like a sturdy phone book or just stand slightly further back from the bed) to give the bag more clearance.

The "Back-Health" Check
If you feel a "pinch" in your lower back during this:

Check your hips: Are they tucked? (Posterior Pelvic Tilt).

Check your height: If the bed is too low, you might be rounding your back. Try propping your supporting hand up on a couple of firm pillows to bring your torso higher.

Would you like me to suggest a specific weight-filler for your suitcase (like water bottles or books) to help you guestimate how much "load" you're actually pulling?

You said
should my back be paralell to the floor or other?

For your height (6'6") and your specific lower back "dullness," the answer is: Nearly parallel, but slightly inclined.

Think of a 15 
∘
  to 20 
∘
  incline—meaning your shoulders should be just slightly higher than your hips.

Why not perfectly parallel?
While "parallel to the floor" is the textbook gold standard for lat activation, it is very high-risk for someone with Anterior Pelvic Tilt (APT) and a long torso.

The Physics: At 6'6", your upper body acts like a very long lever. If you go perfectly parallel, gravity is pulling your spine into a "bridge" shape. This puts massive shear force on those lower vertebrae where you feel that dull pain.

The Fix: By keeping your shoulders slightly higher than your hips, you make it much easier to keep your tailbone tucked and your core engaged.

The "6'6" Suitcase Row" Form Checklist:
The "Hinge" over the "Squat": Push your hips back until you feel a slight stretch in your hamstrings. This "loads" your legs so your back doesn't have to do all the work.

The Support Hand: Ensure the hand on the bed is directly under your shoulder. This creates a "tripod" of stability (Left Hand, Left Foot, Right Foot).

The Neck: Look at a spot on the floor about 2 feet in front of you. Do not look at the mirror or the wall. Looking up will "kink" your neck and pull your lower back into an arch (aggravating your APT).

The "Anti-Sag": Imagine there is a bowl of water resting on your lower back. If you arch too much (APT), the water spills forward. If you round too much, it spills backward. Keep the bowl level.

How to tell if your angle is right:
If you feel it in your lower back: Your torso is likely too flat (too parallel) or you are rounding your spine. Raise your chest slightly.

If you feel it in your traps (top of shoulders): Your torso is likely too upright (too vertical). Lean forward more.

The Sweet Spot: You should feel your middle back (between the shoulder blades) and your lats (under the armpit) doing the work.

Your Workout Final Review:
Go for 4 sets of 15. If the suitcase is light, remember that 3-second squeeze at the top. This is better for your back than moving a heavy weight with momentum.

Once you finish this workout, let me know if the "Under Table" style (the Suitcase Row) felt better or worse on your back than the Barbell Rows you did last week.

You said
is my full arm supposed to be straight?

At the bottom of the movement (the "start"), yes—your arm should be fully extended toward the floor to get a deep stretch in the lat.

However, there is a huge caveat for your height and back pain:

1. The "Active" Hang (Don't "Dead" Hang)
While your arm should be straight, your shoulder should not be "disengaged."

The Wrong Way: Letting the weight of the suitcase pull your shoulder socket down toward the floor so your upper back rounds. This "yanks" on the nerves and ligaments in your neck and middle back.

The Right Way: Keep your shoulder blade "packed." Even with a straight arm, imagine you are pulling your shoulder away from your ear and "pinching" it slightly toward your spine. Your arm is straight, but your back is tense.

2. The Elbow "Soft" Stop
When you pull the suitcase up:

The Top: Your arm should not be straight (obviously), but you also shouldn't pull so high that your elbow goes way past your torso.

The 90-Degree Rule: Stop when your elbow is slightly behind your ribs. If you pull further, your shoulder will "roll forward," which is bad for your posture.

3. The "Long Lever" Reality
Because your arms are so long, the "straight" position at the bottom puts the suitcase very close to the floor.

If the suitcase is hitting the floor before your arm is straight, do not round your back to go lower. * Instead, stand on a couple of sturdy towels or just accept a slightly shorter range of motion. Protecting the flat line of your spine is more important than a fully straight arm.

Summary of the "Arm Path":
Start: Arm straight, shoulder blade "packed" (not shrugging).

Pull: Elbow drives back toward the hip.

Finish: Elbow just past the ribs, forearm vertical, shoulder blade squeezed toward the center of your back.

Return: Control the weight back down until the arm is straight again.

How heavy is the suitcase you’re using? If it's over 40 lbs, be extra careful about that "active" shoulder at the bottom so you don't strain your neck.

You said
do I pull for suitcases the arm up that is opposite from the leg that is forward or visa versa?

You want your opposite leg forward.

If you are rowing with your Right Arm, your Left Leg should be forward.

Why the "Opposite" stance?
Stability: This creates a wide, diagonal "tripod" base between your support hand (on the bed), your front foot, and your back foot. If you put the same leg forward, you’ll be wobbly and likely to tip toward the suitcase.

Pelvic Alignment: For someone with APT and back pain, the opposite-leg-forward stance "locks" your pelvis into a neutral position. It prevents your hips from twisting, which protects your lower spine from that "dull" ache.

The Setup Checklist (Right Arm Row):
Support: Left hand firm on the bed/desk.

Front Foot: Left foot forward, knee slightly bent.

Back Foot: Right foot back (this opens up space so the suitcase doesn't hit your leg).

The Row: Pull the suitcase toward your Right Hip.

Form Check:
As you pull, imagine you are trying to keep your belly button pointing straight at the floor. If your torso starts to rotate so your belly button points toward the wall, you've lost your core engagement. Squeeze your abs harder!

How did the first set of rows feel with the suitcase? Did you find a good "filler" to make it heavy enough?

You said
What do you mean shrugging for overhead press?

When I say "shrug" at the top of the Overhead Press, I don’t mean a standard trap shrug where you pull your shoulders to your ears at the start. I mean a final reach once the bar is already above your head.

The "Active Shoulder" Finish
At 6'6", your arms are very long. When most people lock out their elbows, they stop there. For you, it’s safer to go one inch further:

The Lockout: Push the bar up until your elbows are straight.

The Reach: Once the elbows are locked, imagine you are trying to touch the ceiling with the bar.

The Result: Your shoulder blades (scapulae) will naturally rotate upward and "shrug" slightly.

Why this is vital for your 6'6" frame:
Shoulder Safety: This "shrug" creates space in your shoulder joint (the subacromial space). It prevents the bone from "pinching" your rotator cuff tendons.

Stability: It "locks" the weight into your upper back rather than letting it just hang on your joint sockets.

Back Protection: By reaching high, you encourage your rib cage to stay down and your core to stay tight, which prevents that dull back pain caused by arching mid-press.

The "Window" Check
Remember: At the peak of that shrug/reach, your ears should be slightly in front of your biceps. You are "pushing your head through the window."

Quick Drill: Stand up now with no weight. Reach both hands to the ceiling as high as you possibly can. Feel how your shoulder blades move up? That is the feeling you want at the top of every 55 lb rep.

Given your current progression—specifically your recent jump to 145 lbs on the floor press and 105 lbs on glute bridges—you are approaching a "transition phase." At 6'6" and 207 lbs, your levers are long, so we have to be more calculated with changes than a shorter lifter would be.

Here is the projected timeline for your program evolution:

1. The "Floor-to-Bench" Transition (3–5 Weeks)
We are currently using the Floor Press to protect your shoulders and back.

When to change: Once you can comfortably hit 165 lbs for 4x10 (approx. 80% of your body weight) with zero back pain.

The Change: We will move from the floor to the Weight Bench. This increases the range of motion (the bar goes lower), which is a new challenge for your long arms. We will likely drop the weight back to 135 lbs for the first session on the bench to ensure your shoulders handle the deeper stretch.

2. The "Vertical Pull" Evolution (2–4 Weeks)
You are currently doing Pull-up Negatives.

When to change: When you can consistently perform 3 sets of 5 negatives with a 10-second controlled descent.

The Change: We will introduce Assisted Pull-ups (using a heavy band) or your first Full Chin-ups (palms facing you). At 207 lbs, a full pull-up is an elite feat of strength for someone your height.

3. Step-ups to Split Squats (4–6 Weeks)
The Step-ups on the wooden box are great for stability, but eventually, you'll run out of "balance" challenge.

When to change: When you can do 3x12 per side with a 3-second descent perfectly balanced.

The Change: We will move to Bulgarian Split Squats (back foot elevated on the box). This is the "gold standard" for 6'6" leg training because it builds massive glute power while being much safer for the spine than a traditional back squat.

4. Overhead Press to Standing Barbell (Ongoing)
You are currently doing 55 lbs with your back against the post.

When to change: When you hit 65–70 lbs for 3x12.

The Change: We will move you away from the post to a free-standing Overhead Press. This will be the ultimate test for your back. If the "dull pain" stays at 0/10, this will be your new permanent shoulder builder.

Summary of Additions/Removals:
Additions: We will eventually add a "Hinge" movement (like a Romanian Deadlift) once we are 100% sure your APT is corrected and your back is "bulletproof."

Removals: The Deadbugs and RKC Planks will eventually be phased out once your core is strong enough that your "big lifts" (Presses/Bridges) provide enough stimulus on their own.

You said
fOR THE SCAPULAR WALL SLIDES, what points of contact should my body have against the wall?

Since you are 6'6" with those long levers, getting the wall slide right is the difference between a simple stretch and a high-level postural correction.

For a successful slide that hits your back width and shoulder stability goals, you should have five primary points of constant contact against the wall:

1. The Five Points of Contact
Tailbone (Sacrum): Your glutes should be firmly against the wall.

Upper Back (Thoracic Spine): Your shoulder blades should stay flat.

Back of the Head: Keep your chin tucked (look straight ahead), not tilted up.

Elbows: Both elbows must stay glued to the wall throughout the entire range.

Wrists/Back of Hands: This is the hardest part. Try to keep the back of your hands touching the wall.

2. The "Long-Spine" Set-up
The Feet: Stand with your feet about 6–12 inches away from the wall. This makes it much easier to keep your lower back flat.

The Lower Back "Gap": This is the secret for Goal #1 (Fixing APT). Try to flatten your lower back so there is as little space as possible between your spine and the wall. Think "tailbone tuck."

3. The Movement
Start with your arms in a "W" shape.

Slowly slide your hands up until they form a "Y" shape above your head.

The Rule: If your wrists or elbows pull off the wall, stop there. That is your current "end range." Don't force it—as your chest opens up and your back gets wider, you'll be able to go higher.

Why this matters for today’s 145 lb Floor Press:
By pinning your elbows and wrists to the wall, you are forcing the Lower Traps and Serratus Anterior to wake up. These are the muscles that keep your shoulders stable when you’re pressing heavy weight.

Pro Tip: Since you're at 207 lbs and "barely noticeable" on the back pain, focus on that lower back contact. If you can keep your lower back flat against the wall while sliding your arms up, you are effectively "training" your body out of Anterior Pelvic Tilt.

Give a few reps a try—do your elbows stay against the wall easily, or do they want to pop off as soon as you reach shoulder height?

You said
I see to lack the flexibility for this move. If I have my tailbone and upper back and back of head against the wall, my elbows can only make contact at the bottom of the move and my wrists/back of hands can never make contact

That is actually incredibly common, especially for someone 6'6" with a history of APT. It’s usually a sign that your chest (pecs) and the muscles that rotate your shoulders are tight, which is a classic side effect of a long wingspan and working long hours on your feet.

Since you can't hit those points of contact yet, do not force it. Forcing it will just make your lower back arch off the wall, which works against your goal of fixing your pelvic tilt.

The "Step-Down" Alternative: Floor Slides
If the wall is too punishing, we move to the floor. Gravity becomes your assistant instead of your opponent.

Lay on your back on the floor (like you’re getting ready for Deadbugs).

Knees bent, feet flat. Flatten your lower back into the ground (tuck the tailbone).

The "W" Position: Place your arms on the floor in that "W" shape.

The Goal: Use the weight of your arms to let your elbows and the back of your hands sink toward the floor.

The Slide: Slowly slide them up. If they lift off, stop, take a breath, and slide back down.

Why this is actually good news for your goals:
This "lack of flexibility" is exactly why we are focusing on Goal #3 (Back Width) and Goal #2 (Chest Definition).

Tight pecs are "hiding" your chest definition by pulling your shoulders forward.

Tight lats are preventing you from getting the full range of motion needed to build that "V-taper" width.

Today’s Warmup Adjustment:
Since the wall slides are a no-go for now, replace them with:

Doorway Chest Stretch: 1 minute per side. This is now mandatory before your 145 lb Floor Press today. It will "unlock" your shoulders so you can actually use your chest muscles during the press.

You said
pull my elbows towards the back or side wall?

For the Inverted Barbell Rows, since your goal is Goal #3: Back Width and the "V-Taper," you want to pull your elbows toward the side walls.

Here is the breakdown of why that distinction matters for your 6'6" frame:

1. The "Side Wall" Pull (For Back Width)
The Movement: As you pull yourself up to the bar, imagine you are trying to "elbow" two people standing directly to your left and right.

The Result: This flares the Lats and engages the Teres Major (the "little lat"). This is the muscle that creates that specific width just under your armpits.

The Cue: Think "Wide Elbows."

2. The "Back Wall" Pull (For Back Thickness)
The Movement: Pulling the elbows straight back and tucking them tight to your ribs.

The Result: This hits the Rhomboids and mid-traps (the muscles between your shoulder blades). It makes your back look "thick" from the side, but doesn't contribute as much to the "V" look from the front.

Your Strategy for Today (Notch 25):
Since we are aiming for 4x10 with that 3-second hold on the first rep:

Grip: Take a grip that is slightly wider than your shoulders.

The Pull: Drive your elbows out toward the side walls.

The Chest: Pull until your chest almost touches the bar.

The Hold: On that first rep, when your chest is at the bar and your elbows are flared toward the sides, squeeze for 3 seconds. You should feel a massive "stretch" and "tightness" across the top of your back and under your armpits.

Why this helps Goal #1 (APT):
By pulling with "Wide Elbows," you are forced to keep your core tighter to stay stable. If you let your back arch, your elbows will naturally want to tuck. Keeping them wide helps "lock" your ribs down and your pelvis in place.

Ready to attack those rows? Remember: 207 lbs is a lot of weight to pull, so if you hit 8 reps with perfect "Side Wall" form, that’s better than 10 reps with tucked elbows!

You said
I think I'm feeling the negatives more in my biceps and forearms. Does that mean I'm not doing this right?

Actually, that’s a very common experience for tall lifters, especially when moving to a 10-second descent. While we want the lats to do the heavy lifting, your biceps and forearms are the "weakest links" in the chain, so they often scream the loudest.

Here is why you’re feeling it there and how we can shift the focus back to your back width (Goal #3):

1. The "Long Lever" Effect
At 6'6", your forearms and upper arms are long. This creates a massive amount of "torque" on your elbow and wrist joints. Your forearms have to work overtime just to keep your 207 lb frame attached to the bar for 10 full seconds.

2. The "Hook" vs. The "Grip"
If you are squeezing the bar like you’re trying to choke it, your forearms and biceps will take over.

The Fix: Try using a "thumbless grip" (suicide grip) where your thumb stays on the same side of the bar as your fingers. Imagine your hands are just hooks and your elbows are doing the pulling.

3. The "Elbow-First" Descent
If you feel it mostly in your biceps, you might be "curling" your way down.

The Fix: As you descend, focus on driving your elbows down and out toward your hips. Imagine you are trying to put your elbows into your back pockets. This engages the lats (the width muscles) and takes the strain off the bicep.

4. Is it "Wrong"?
No. Your biceps and forearms have to work to hold 207 lbs. Feeling them means they are getting stronger. However, if you feel zero tightness or fatigue in your back/under your armpits, then we need to adjust.

The "Mind-Muscle" Drill for Next Time:
Before your first negative on Wednesday, do this:

Stand up and reach for the ceiling.

Pull your shoulders down away from your ears (depress the scapula).

Tighten the area right under your armpits (where the lats are).

Hold that tightness as you jump up to the bar for the negative.

Recovery Note:
Since your forearms and biceps took a beating, you might feel some "tightness" in the crook of your elbow tomorrow.

Stretch: Extend your arm out, palm up, and gently pull your fingers back toward your floor.

Massage: Use your thumb to rub out the meat of your forearm.

The "Mid-Point Pause": Most people "drop" the last 3 inches. Try to make the very bottom of the move (the last 20%) the slowest part.

You said
Remind me how to hold my body during the negatives to align with my focuses

Since you’re weighing in at 207 lbs today and managing a 3/10 back ache, your form on the Pull-up Negatives needs to be "surgical." At 6'6", your long levers mean any misalignment puts massive stress on your spine and elbows.

Here is exactly how to hold your body to hit Goal #3 (Back Width) and Goal #1 (Fixing APT):

1. The "Anti-APT" Lower Body (The Anchor)
If you let your legs dangle loosely, your weight will pull your lower back into a deep arch, aggravating that 3/10 pain.

The "Crunch": Before you jump up, pull your ribcage down toward your belt buckle.

The "Leg Lock": Squeeze your legs together and point your toes slightly forward (hollow body position). This keeps your pelvis "tucked" and prevents the weight of your 207 lb frame from "hanging" on your spine.

2. The "Back Width" Upper Body (The Wing-Span)
To shift the focus away from your biceps and toward your lats:

The "Pinky Grip": Squeeze the bar hardest with your pinky and ring fingers. This neurologically activates the lats better than a "death grip" with the index finger.

Elbows to Side Walls: As you descend, don’t just pull your elbows down; try to flared them out toward the side walls. Imagine you are trying to bend the bar into a horseshoe shape.

Chest Up: Keep your collarbone high. If you "slouch" or round your shoulders forward, the biceps will take over and you'll lose the "V-taper" stimulus.

3. The "10-Second" Descent Strategy
Because of your height, the distance is long. Break it into three "zones":

Top 1/3 (The Squeeze): Fight like crazy to stay at the very top for the first 3 seconds. This builds the "shelf" of your upper back.

Middle 1/3 (The Width): This is where the lats do the most work. Keep the elbows flared. (Seconds 4-7).

Bottom 1/3 (The Control): Crucial for your back. Most people "drop" the last 2 inches, which shocks the spine. Fight for those last 3 seconds (8, 9, 10) until your arms are fully extended.

The "Back-Check" Protocol
If at any point during the 10 seconds you feel a "twinge" or "pull" in your lower back:

Immediately drop the count to 5–7 seconds.

The goal today is Back Width, not "Survival." If your form breaks, the muscle-building stimulus disappears.

Ready to jump up? Remember: Ribs down, pinkies tight, and drive those elbows toward the Gilbert horizon!

You said
And what is the grip I am supposed to use?

For your Pull-up Negatives, since the goal is Back Width (Goal #3) while protecting your 3/10 back ache, you should use an Overhand Grip (Pronated).

Here is the specific setup for your 6'6" frame:

1. The Width: "Slightly Wider than Shoulders"
The Sweet Spot: Place your hands about 2–3 inches wider than your actual shoulder width.

Why: At your height, going too wide puts your shoulders in a vulnerable position and shortens your range of motion. Going too narrow (shoulder-width or less) turns the move into a "bicep exercise." A moderate-wide grip forces the lats to flare, which builds that "V-taper" width.

2. The Style: "The Thumbless Hook" (Suicide Grip)
The Setup: Instead of wrapping your thumb around the bar, place your thumb on top of the bar, alongside your fingers.

Why: This is a "cheat code" for people who feel negatives in their biceps and forearms. It turns your hands into "hooks." It makes it much harder to "curl" your way down and forces the tension into the large muscles under your armpits (the lats).

3. The "Pinky-Pressure" Cue
Regardless of how you wrap your fingers, imagine you are trying to snap the bar by pulling down with your pinky and ring fingers. This small mental shift "turns on" the lats instantly.

Summary Checklist for your Grip:
[ ] Overhand (Palms facing away from you).

[ ] Width (Just outside your shoulders).

[ ] Thumbless (If you want to shift the burn away from your biceps).

[ ] Pinky Drive (Apply pressure with the outside of your hand).

A Note on the 3/10 Back Pain:
If an overhand grip feels too "stiff" for your shoulders or causes you to arch your back to compensate, you can switch to a Neutral Grip (palms facing each other) if your bar allows it. This is generally the "kindest" grip for the spine and shoulders.

How does that "thumbless" hook feel when you test it out? Does it help you feel that "under-armpit" connection more?

It hasn't been "wrong"—it has actually been the reason you've been able to progress so safely despite the APT. Here is why we should stay with your current narrow, neutral grip:

1. The "Neutral" Advantage for Your Back
With your back at a 3/10, the neutral grip (palms facing each other) is much safer. It keeps your shoulders in a "packed" position and makes it significantly easier to keep your ribs down and tailbone tucked. An overhand wide grip often forces tall lifters to arch their back—which is exactly what we want to avoid right now.

2. Why "Narrower than Shoulders" Works for You
At 6'6", your "narrow" is still quite a bit of distance! Using a grip slightly inside shoulder width with palms facing each other:

Targets the Lower Lats: This helps pull the "bottom" of the V-shape, which supports the spine.

You said
SO what about thumbless grip and flaring elbows?

Since we are sticking with the Neutral Grip (palms facing each other) and a narrower width, we need to adjust those cues to fit that specific movement. Using "wide-grip cues" on a "narrow-grip move" would be like trying to drive a truck with a motorcycle handle—it just won't feel right.

Here is how to apply those concepts to your current setup:

1. The "Thumbless" Grip: YES
You should still try the thumbless grip even with your palms facing each other.

How: Wrap your four fingers around the handle, but keep your thumb on the same side as your fingers (don't wrap it under).

The Benefit: This is the #1 way to solve your "I feel it in my biceps" problem. It stops you from "squeezing" the life out of the bar, which forces the tension to travel up into your lats (the width muscles).

2. Flaring Elbows: NO
With a neutral, narrow grip, you should NOT flare your elbows out to the side walls.

The Correct Cue: Instead of "flaring," think about tucking your elbows toward your ribs.

The Benefit: Flaring your elbows with a narrow, neutral grip puts awkward "twisting" pressure on your shoulders. By driving your elbows down and slightly back (toward your back pockets), you engage the lower lats, which helps stabilize your 6'6" spine and protects that 3/10 back ache.

The "Updated" Checklist for your 10-Second Negatives:
Grip: Palms facing each other, narrow width.

Thumb: Keep it on the same side as your fingers (The Hook).

Elbows: Drive them down toward your hips, not out to the sides.

Core (The APT Fix): Pull your ribcage down and squeeze your legs together.

Why this is better for your 3/10 Back:
Tucking the elbows while using a neutral grip makes it much easier to keep your core "engaged." If you tried to flare your elbows in a narrow position, your ribs would likely flare up, your back would arch, and that 3/10 pain could spike.

90/90 Breathing (5 mins): Feet up on a chair/couch, lower back flat on the floor. Focus on deep belly breaths.

Cat-Cow (10 slow reps): Small, gentle movements to "oil" the spine.

Bird-Dog (10 reps per side): Focus on a perfectly flat back; imagine a glass of water on your lower back that you can't spill.

Doorway Chest Stretch (1 min per side): Open the pecs to ensure your shoulders don't pull forward during the press.

Floor Scapular Slides (10 reps): Keep your elbows and the back of your hands touching the floor the whole time.

Part 2: The Workout (Workout A)
Exercise	Target	The "6'6" Form Cues
Barbell Floor Press	145 lbs / 4x8-10	Wider Grip. Glue your lower back to the floor. Imagine "pinching" your chest together at the top.
Inverted Barbell Row	Notch 25 / 4x8-10	Side Wall Elbows. Pull your elbows out wide to the sides to hit the upper back/width.
Tempo Pushups	3x8	3-Sec Descent. If the back feels tight, do these on the floor instead of an incline.
Pull-up Negatives	3x5 / 10-sec	Neutral Grip + Thumbless. Elbows tucked to ribs. Squeeze legs together to protect the spine.
Part 3: The "Negatives" Execution (Summary)
Since we cleared up the confusion, here is the exact "Neutral Grip" setup:

Grip: Palms facing each other, narrow/shoulder-width.

The Hook: Use a Thumbless Grip (thumb on the same side as fingers) to take the stress off your biceps and forearms.

The Path: Drive your elbows down toward your back pockets, not out to the sides.

The Count: 10 slow seconds. If your back feels a "twinge," drop to 7 seconds immediately.

Part 4: Post-Workout Recovery
Refeed: You’re at 207 lbs. Aim for 2,900 calories today with 200g of protein.

Decompress: After your workout, spend 2 minutes hanging from the pull-up bar (feet touching the floor for support) to stretch out the spine.

Monitor: If the 3/10 pain increases, we will take a full 48-hour break. If it decreases, the movement did its job!

You said
how to do a glute bridge while ensuring the focus is on your glutes and not your low back

To perform a glute bridge without straining your lower back, you must eliminate the "arch" in your spine and replace it with a Posterior Pelvic Tilt (PPT). For someone with Anterior Pelvic Tilt (APT), the lower back naturally wants to take over the movement.

1. The Setup: The "Rib-to-Hip" Connection
Before you lift your hips, you must set your spine.

The Flat Back: Lie on your back with knees bent. Forcefully exhale and press your lower back into the floor so there is zero gap between your spine and the ground.

Ribs Down: Imagine pulling your ribcage down toward your belt buckle. This engages your abs, which act as a "brake" to prevent your lower back from arching during the lift.

2. The Initiation: The "Tailbone Tuck"
Drive Through Heels: Focus your weight on your heels, not your toes.

Tuck the Tail: Instead of just "pushing up," think about curling your tailbone toward your chin. This ensures the movement starts from the glutes rather than the lumbar spine.

3. The Execution: The "Short" Bridge
Stop Early: Most people experience back pain because they try to push their hips as high as possible. For those with APT, the top 10% of the movement is often just a lower back arch.

The Straight Line: Only lift until your knees, hips, and shoulders form a straight line. If you feel a "pinch" in your back, you have gone too high.

The Squeeze: At the top, squeeze your glutes as hard as possible, as if you are trying to crush a coin between your butt cheeks.

4. The Descent: Rolling Down
Don't drop your hips all at once. Imagine your spine is a string of pearls; lay it back down onto the floor one pearl at a time, starting from the upper back and ending with the tailbone.

Reset your "lower back flat" position before starting the next rep.

Troubleshooting
Hamstrings taking over? Move your feet slightly closer to your glutes.

Quads taking over? Move your feet slightly further away.

Still feel the back? Reduce the range of motion. Even a 2-inch lift done with a perfect "tuck" is more effective for your goals than a high lift done with an arched back.

Would you like me to adjust your targets for Monday's 115 lb glute bridges to focus on a shorter range of motion to ensure we stay at 0/10 pain?

You said
YEs

Since we are keeping the weight at 115 lbs for Monday’s Workout B, we will use a "Quality over Quantity" approach. To protect your back while weighing 210 lbs, we will implement the "Short Bridge" method.

The Monday "No-Back" Bridge Protocol
The Exhale Reset: Before every single rep, blow all your air out. This forced exhale drops your ribs and pins your lower back to the floor. Do not start the rep until you feel that floor connection.

The 80% Height Rule: Instead of aiming for maximum height, stop your hips about 2 inches lower than you think you should.

The Check: If you can’t maintain a hard "tailbone tuck" at the top, you’ve gone too high.

The 2-Second "Crush": At the top of each rep, focus entirely on the glute squeeze. Imagine you are trying to shorten the distance between your hamstrings and your lower back using only your glutes.

The Feet-to-Floor Connection: Spread the floor apart with your feet without actually moving them. This "lateral tension" helps keep the glutes engaged and prevents the lower back from taking the load.

Updated Monday Workout B Plan
Based on 210 lbs body weight and 1/10 back ache.

Lateral Raises	5 lbs / 3x15-20	Control: Don't use momentum; make the side delts do the work.
Deadbugs	3x15 per side	Pinned Spine: Zero gap between back and floor.
RKC Plank	3x45s	Max Tension: Focus on the "shake," not just the time.

You said
how close should your heels be to your glutes in the bridges?

For your specific goals—minimizing lower back pain and maximizing glute engagement—the ideal distance is usually about 6 to 9 inches away from your glutes, or roughly where your fingertips can just barely graze your heels when your arms are flat at your sides.

However, since everyone’s proportions are different (especially at 6'6"), you should use these "feel" markers to find your sweet spot:

1. The "Shin" Test (Ideal Placement)
When you are at the top of the bridge, your shins should be vertical (perpendicular to the floor). This creates the most direct line of force for the glutes to drive the weight.

2. If feet are too close (Too much Quad)
If your heels are tucked right up against your butt:

You will feel the "burn" in the front of your thighs (Quads).

It becomes much harder to perform the "Tailbone Tuck" (PPT), which often causes the lower back to arch to compensate for the cramped space.

3. If feet are too far away (Too much Hamstring)
If your legs are extended too far out:

Your Hamstrings will try to take over the lift.

This often leads to hamstring cramping and makes it difficult to get a deep "squeeze" in the glutes at the top.

The "6'6" Adjustment" for Today
Because you have longer levers, you might find that having your feet slightly wider than hip-width and turned out about 15 degrees (duck feet) allows you to "tuck" your pelvis more effectively.

Try this during your warmup:

Set your feet where your shins are vertical at the top.

Perform one rep.

If you feel your lower back working, move your feet half an inch further away and try again.

Does shifting your feet slightly further away help you feel that "Short Bridge" glute squeeze more clearly?

You said
Actually, I found that I may have been having my feet too far away and this was engaging my lower back. And when I moved them closer, it seemed to more directly target my glutes

That adjustment is a critical find. For lifters with longer femurs (thigh bones), having the feet too far away often forces the pelvis into an anterior tilt to bridge the gap, which is exactly why your lower back was taking the load.

By moving your heels closer, you’ve shortened the lever, making it mechanically easier to maintain that Posterior Pelvic Tilt (PPT) and lock the tension into the glutes.

Technical Adjustment for Today (Feb 16)
The "Heel-to-Glute" Distance: Keep your heels at that closer distance where you felt the direct glute connection during your test.

Vertical Shin Check: Aim for your shins to be vertical (90 degrees to the floor) at the very top of the bridge. If they are angled toward your head, your feet might be too close (quad dominant); if they angle away, they are too far (hamstring/back dominant).

The "Short Bridge" Height: Even with the better foot placement, still stop 1–2 inches shy of your absolute maximum height. This ensures the 115 lbs stays on the glutes and doesn't "leak" into the lumbar spine.

Key Form Adjustments
Based on the video's "Golden Rules" [00:58], here is how to stabilize the bar and protect your back:

This creates a stable "shelf" and removes the need for your shoulders to "fight" the roll.

Pack Your Shoulders: Just like a bench press, you need to tightly pack your shoulder blades down and back before you start [01:22]. This creates a solid foundation against the floor so your upper body doesn't slide when you drive the weight up.

For a tall person, especially with longer legs, specific technical adjustments in the glute bridge are necessary to avoid lower back strain. The following video details "Golden Rules" that help stabilize the movement.

Key Adjustments for Tall Lifters
Bar Stability: Tall lifters often struggle with the bar rolling back toward the torso. To fix this, lock your arms out and manually push the barbell into your thighs [04:17]. This keeps the weight off your hip bones and prevents it from shifting toward your face.

Foot Placement: It is critical to keep your heels close to your glutes [01:28]. As you lift heavier, your feet may naturally slide forward every few reps [02:53]. If they move too far away, the load transfers from your glutes to your hamstrings and lower back [03:00]. Stop and reset your heels closer to your glutes if you feel this shift [03:19].

Core Bracing: To protect the spine, take a deep breath and hold it to brace your core before every rep [03:26].

You said
should I have my back on a bench or the floor?

To address the specific goal of fixing Anterior Pelvic Tilt (APT) and protecting your lower back, the floor is the superior choice for your current training phase.

Here is the analytical breakdown of why the floor is more effective for your specific mechanics:

1. The Floor (Glute Bridge)
Built-in Safety: The floor acts as a physical stop. It is impossible to "over-arch" your back downward, which makes it much easier to keep your spine neutral.

Posterior Pelvic Tilt (PPT): It is easier to feel your lower back "pin" against the floor. This feedback is critical for ensuring your glutes are doing the work and not your lumbar spine.

Stability: At 6'6", a bench can sometimes feel unstable or too narrow for your frame. The floor provides a completely stable foundation for your shoulders.

2. The Bench (Hip Thrust)
Increased Range of Motion: Because your hips start lower, you move through a larger range. While good for muscle growth, this significantly increases the risk of your back arching at the bottom or the top.

Pivot Point Issues: For a tall person, if the bench is not the exact right height, it can act as a fulcrum that actually encourages your lower back to extension (arching), which is exactly what we are trying to avoid.

Recommendation for Today’s Workout B
Stay on the floor. Using the floor while implementing the "Short Bridge" (stopping 2 inches before the top) and the "Close Heel" placement you discovered will give you the highest degree of glute isolation with the lowest risk to your back.

Summary of Setup:

Surface: Floor.

Back: Shoulders packed tight against the ground.

Focus: Forcefully pinning the lower back to the floor before every rep.

Strategy: The "Thumbless Hook" for Workout A
Since you are at 206 lbs today and the back is at a 1/10, the goal for tomorrow's Pull-up Negatives is to ensure the latissimus dorsi (back) takes the full weight of your 6'6" frame, rather than your biceps or forearms.

The Technical Cues:

The Grip: Place your thumb on the same side as your fingers. This removes the ability to "squeeze" with the thumb, which naturally shuts down some of the forearm/bicep compensation.

The "Elbow Tuck": As you descend, imagine you are trying to put your elbows into your back pockets. This keeps the tension on the lats.

The 10-Second Count: Do not rush the first 3 seconds. The most critical part for your back health is the very top of the movement where the lats are fully contracted.

Nutrition: You hit 209 lbs post-eating/post-workout. This is a solid "fueled" weight. Keep the 2,900 kcal consistent over the weekend to recover from the 125 lb load.

Spine Care: The 125 lb bridge is a lot of compression. Spend 5–10 minutes in 90/90 Breathing tonight to ensure you don't wake up with a 3/10 ache tomorrow.

Monday Prep (Workout A): Since the 60 lb OHP went well, we will look to push the Floor Press back toward that 145 lb x 40 goal.
